Fock-space. Such basis is related with a point process, with the number
of points with a given feature corresponding to the number of
modifications to the uniform prior (in the part of the sample space
corresponding to such feature). Since Bayesian inference with any other
prior can be seen as a combination of the results of different Bayesian
inferences with the uniform prior for different data (eventually an
infinite amount of data for the cases with null measure), then the
uniform prior in the free field parametrization is appropriate for
non-deterministic functions in the absence of any other information.

\hypertarget{application-digital-first-mathematics-and-mathematical-modelling}{%
\section{10. Application: Digital-first Mathematics and Mathematical
Modelling}\label{application-digital-first-mathematics-and-mathematical-modelling}}

Most mathematics and mathematical modelling are human-first (or
analog-first or book-first). That is, the framework is optimized such
that the computers are dispensable, computers play a mere productivity
role. For instance, a mathematics book is such that it could be written
by hand, but by writing it in the computer it can reach students faster
and cheaper. The same happens with most mathematical models of complex
systems: they can in principle be written by hand, the computer just
increases productivity.

But in the next few years, mathematics and mathematical modelling will
become digital-first. That is, the framework is optimized such that the
human interaction is dispensable, humans play a mere auditing role. For
instance, we could have new alternatives to complex analysis that are
born digital. As it happens today with digital photos, humans are
provided with an analog view (in a screen or on paper) of the photo and
check if they like it, but the photo itself is digital, and it has more
information/resolution than what humans can easily distinguish. As it is
progressively happening with autonomous driving, where humans audit the
car's autonomous progress but don't effectively drive as much.

Bayesian inference (or approximations to it, such as deep neural
networks) is not deterministic. Therefore, Machine Learning can create
deterministic mathematics, but it is not deterministic mathematics
itself. Thus, we still need some kind of mathematics. All current
Computer Algebra Systems and Proof assistants were optimized for a
human-first Mathematics. They are computationally inefficient, verbose,
logically inconsistent, designed for a human-first mathematics and to
please the human user.

An alternative is the Haskell library Egison (in its sweet-egison
implementation\cite{egi_functional_2020}, a similar alternative would be
Metamath\cite{polu_generative_2020}). Haskell is a pure functional language (thus easy to run in
parallel architectures, see \url{www.acceleratehs.org}) and it is the
only pure functional language that is often as fast as C. Moreover, we
can easily include C code in Haskell in it in the exceptional cases
where C is much faster. Then the Haskell's Egison library allows us to
implement (probably) the most expressive and advanced Computer Algebra
System. Thus, we have expressiveness, flexibility and speed, despite
that it requires a lot of human expertise to do Mathematics from scratch
with Haskell's Egison (which is ok for Digital-first Mathematics). Since
the human user plays an auditing role, another language such as Python
would not be much more useful than Haskell (a complex Python function is
also hard to audit by a non-expert, moreover machine learning knows how
to write Haskell interfaces to the Python libraries that could
eventually be needed) and we don't need many human auditors in
Mathematics (due to its Universality) in the World, these few people can
be specialists in Haskell, Egison and Mathematics.

Most human users will see/edit views of the Haskell code (constraints
/explanations in plain English for instance), but not the code itself.
These views are non-deterministic and can be a source of errors, which
are ultimately audited by specialists in Haskell and Mathematics.

\hypertarget{application-learning-interesting-questions-for-which-we-can-find-a-predictive-model}{%
\section{11. Application: Learning interesting questions, for which we
can find a predictive
model}\label{application-learning-interesting-questions-for-which-we-can-find-a-predictive-model}}

Perhaps the biggest advantage of defining deep learning as a random
sampling method in Bayesian inference, is that it allows us to apply
deep learning (or alternative sampling methods) to more abstract
problems than (eventually unsupervised) machine learning.

We all love to make a prediction and to be right (``I told you''). Thus,
we tend to try to find questions/problems for which we can learn the
answer to. Note that, our memory is limited, so this is one relevant way
(perhaps even the primary way) that allows us to select the information
to save in memory, since the more predictive a model is, the less memory
it requires\cite{deletang_language_2023}. Since we cannot answer everything anyway, we try to find
those questions we can answer to, to allocate scarce resources to them.

So we have a hierarchical inference problem, where the computation of
the posterior probability includes another inference problem. And the
hierarchy can have as many levels as we wish. But there is only one
human brain or only one machine, so it does not make much sense to say
that an inference problem in an intermediate level of the hierarchy is
``machine learning'' or a ``neural network'' analogous to the brain.
However, once we define deep learning as a random sampling method in
Bayesian inference, then it makes perfect sense to apply it to the
intermediate levels of the hierarchy, since a hierarchy of
beliefs/probabilities is a natural concept.

\hypertarget{negotiation-is-an-alternative-to-alignment}{%
\section{12. Negotiation is an alternative to
alignment}\label{negotiation-is-an-alternative-to-alignment}}

How to prevent misinformation online? Incentives to misinform are the
same as for AI (publicity for instance), since the AI is trained by by
humans who use it to as a way to achieve its human goals. So, even with
perfect alignment the AI would still be misleading to end-users. Then it
is also misleading to its trainers indirectly, since every trainer is
the end-user of other AIs.

Incentive to say the truth publicly in Forex is null.

Dependency hell is related to the alignment problem. Because knowledge
is divided in statistically independent modules, interfaced by a router
implementing a mixture of experts. But this structure, when scaled leads
to the dependency hell.

Safety is related to dependency hell, and life is related to survival
which is related to safety. Safety is not abstract, it must be rooted in
the real-world. And thus the best solution to the dependency hell, is
the structure that is more resilient in the real world. And these are
living organisms.

\chapter{Statistical Model Theory and Bayesian priors where the Riemann Hypothesis is true}
\hypertarget{upward-lwenheim-skolem-theorem}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Upward Löwenheim-Skolem
Theorem}}{Upward Löwenheim-Skolem Theorem}}\label{upward-lwenheim-skolem-theorem}}

\textbf{The Theorem:} If a First-Order theory has an infinite model
(which Presburger Arithmetic does, i.e.,te cardinality (uncountable,
etc.).

\textbf{The Consequence:} No matter how many axioms you add (as long as
they are First-Order), if the model allows for an infinite set of
numbers, a "monster" uncountable model that satisfies those exact same
axioms automatically exists.

You cannot filter out the uncountable models using First-Order axioms.
You would need \textbf{Second-Order Logic} to do this, but Second-Order
Logic is not automatable (it is not recursively enumerable).

\hypertarget{statistical-model-theory}{%
\section{Statistical Model Theory}\label{statistical-model-theory}}

Goals: 1) Be able to include uncertain constraints on possible models,
this allows to apply Statistical Model Theory to more realistic
situations, where Model Theory by itself has been insufficient so far
(in the Riemann hypothesis, for example); 2) Be able to define models
which are probabilistic themselves, while preserving completeness; 3) we
can use probabilities in intermediate steps to solve problems which do
not involve probabilities a priori, for instance a) despite being
decidable, Presburger arithmetic has non-standard models (in contrast
with the \(L_{\omega_1,\omega_1}\) theory of Hilbert spaces within
Abstraction logic); b) wave-functions allows to define gradients needed
for gradient descent even if the optimal solution does not involve
probabilities; c) the real numbers can be the unique solution to a set
of axioms, without hurting completeness or compactness. Note that there
are non-standard models of the real numbers in first-order-logic (which
is complete), while in second-order-logic the real numbers are the
unique solution to a set of axioms, but the logic is incomplete.

A separable Hilbert space is fully defined by a countable basis (since
it closure is unique). And the space of all countable models can be made
a standard probability space. This allows us to consider models which
are not countable, but are a Hilbert space with a countable basis (such
as the real numbers vs. rational numbers), while allowing us to define a
standard probability measure in the space of models satisfying a set of
constraints. Such probability measure can be considered as an uncertain
constraint.

Once we define the \(L_{\omega_1,\omega_1}\) theory of Hilbert spaces
within Abstraction logic, we have the implication that there is a a
finite proof (within Abstraction Logic) for every statement or its
negation, since both \(L_{\omega_1,\omega_1}\) logic and Abstraction
Logic are complete.

Then, we can represent statements (theorems if proven so) as operators
in a Hilbert space. This allows to deal with undecidable statements as
projection operators, which project the Hilbert space of models to a
smaller subspace. Statements become more than true/false/undecidable,
since we can study numerically the projection operators (using Krylov
subspace methods, for instance). We can even consider uncertain
statements, that are operators but not projections. A statement is then
the definition of an operator, while its proof is the numerical
implementation corresponding to the definition. In this sense, an
undecidable statement also has a proof and ``approximated'' proofs
(which evaluate incorrectly for a small subspace of the models).

The second incompleteness theorem is only upsetting if the language
allows to represent self-consistency. If it does, then due to
completeness and undecidability of self-consistency, there is at least
one non-standard model (which cannot be separated from the standard
model) where self-consistency is not represented by the original
statement which is a false statement (self-consistency cannot be false
since such non-standard model exists). In other words, the
self-reference is valid in the standard model and invalid in at least
one non-standard model. This makes sense since the mapping necessary for
self-reference uses one specific model (usually the standard model).

If the language does represent self-consistency, that would not be an
improvement over representing it because it could still be inconsistent.
Note that-self-consistency does not add new information because we
already need to assume the consistency of a theory (otherwise would be
no model satisfying the constraints).

\hypertarget{bayesian-priors-where-the-riemann-hypothesis-is-true}{%
\section{Bayesian priors where the Riemann Hypothesis is
true}\label{bayesian-priors-where-the-riemann-hypothesis-is-true}}

It is well known that the Peano arithmetic is Turing complete. This
suggests that it assumes more than needed. Certainly, the minimum
assumptions we want for a mathematical theory are related to those of a
Turing machine, since we want to be able to do any real-world
computation. But more assumptions than those are not crucial, and
therefore should be used only if they are harmless and advantageous for
some reason (make proofs easier, for instance).

The \(L_{\omega_1\omega_1}\)-theory of Hilbert spaces
\cite{kopperman_l-theory_1967} is
harmless, because it has very good properties (completeness,
compactness, etc.), and it makes Bayesian inference possible for
standard probability spaces. It uses infinitary logic, but all models
are separable Hilbert spaces (it is also possible to assume
non-separable Hilbert spaces, but here we choose not to), meaning that
we can use any finitary theory where separable Hilbert spaces are a
model and make more assumptions to get the corresponding
\(L_{\omega_1\omega_1}\)-theory of Hilbert spaces as a particular case.
Then whatever is true or false for all separable Hilbert spaces in the
finitary theory, it is also true or false in the corresponding
infinitary theory. Moreover, due to the compactness property, we do not
loose expressivity when studying a finitary theory dependent on an
arbitrarily large parameter instead of an infinitary theory.

Once we can do Bayesian inference in a harmless way, then we can define
the unknown. And then we can define computation without involving the
infinite (unlike in a Turing machine). Since any real-world computation
requires finite time and energy, the results of some computations that
end in finite time cannot be known for all practical purposes. So, for
instance we only need to define the multiplication of two integers
smaller than some finite large integer, and for larger integers we can
use a Bayesian prior since we will never know the result.

Note that our assumptions include axioms and Bayesian priors, while the
usual assumptions in mathematics only involve axioms. This by itself
should raise a red flag: all scientific knowledge involves Bayesian
priors in one way or another, except for (most of) mathematics. The
reason is that Peano arithmetic by itself already assumes too much,
leaving no room for the unknown. Since no one knows everything, the
unknown still appears in (most of) mathematics, but as a bug
(impossibility of proving consistency of the theory from within, for
instance) not as a feature.

The hypothesis that the Möbius function is related with a random walk
implies the Riemann hypothesis. The Möbius function for arbitrarily
large integers is unknown, for all practical purposes. Thus, we can
choose a reasonable Bayesian prior (that is, the Möbius function is iid
for large integers), relating the Möbius function with a random walk,
implying the Riemann hypothesis. Note that many axioms involving limits
are harmless (Presburger arithmetic involves only sums and it has good
properties, for instance) and thus no need Bayesian priors, but Peano
arithmetic assumes so many axioms that it becomes harmful.

.

Spaces. \emph{The Journal of Symbolic Logic}, \emph{32}(3), 295--304.
